1402 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


Durham v. Lyckan Development Corp.


No. 7514SC215


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


26 N.C. App. 210; 215 S.E.2d 814; 1975 N.C. App. LEXIS 2011


May 14, 1975, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
June 18, 1975, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by plaintiff from order entered by Clark, Judge, on 10 January 1974 and judgment entered by Hall, Judge, on 19 December 1974; and appeal by defendant Lyckan Development Corporation (hereinafter referred to as defendant) from order entered by Clark, Judge, on 15 January 1974, in Superior Court, Durham County.



DISPOSITION: New trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff sought review of the decision of the Durham County Superior Court (North Carolina). Plaintiff argued that the trial court erred in the first proceeding by allowing defendant to make eight separate motions without reference to any rule of civil procedure and in concluding that defendant was not properly served with process. Plaintiff argued in the second proceeding that the trial court erred in excluding a map from evidence.


OVERVIEW: The court found that plaintiff was not prejudiced by defendant's failure to state the rule number under which it filed its motions as required by N.C. Gen. R. Prac. Super. & Dist. Ct. 6. While defendant failed to comply with the trial court rules, it fully complied with N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1A-1, N.C. R. Civ. P. 7(b)(1). The court also found that the trial court did not err in finding that defendant was not properly served under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1A-1, N.C. R. Civ. P. 4(j)(6), which required that a copy be left with an officer or managing agent of the corporation. The trial court found that defendant's president was not in the county when he was allegedly served after business hours. While the evidence might have warranted different findings, the court determined that the evidence supported the trial court's finding. Therefore, the court affirmed the first judgment appealed from. The court reversed the second judgment and ordered a new trial, finding that a map depicting floodway zones was improperly excluded. It was relevant and material because plaintiff contended that the land taken was subject to flooding, and the map would have contained information bearing on the issue.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the first judgment appealed from, and it reversed the second judgment and ordered a new trial on the issue of the amount of damages.

 

1403 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


Nelson v. North Carolina State Board of Alcoholic Control


No. 7510SC258


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


26 N.C. App. 303; 216 S.E.2d 152; 1975 N.C. App. LEXIS 2031


May 29, 1975, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
June 18, 1975, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by respondent from Lee, Judge. Judgment entered 7 February 1975 in Superior Court, Wake County.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the North Carolina State Board of Alcoholic Control, sought review of a judgment of the Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina), which held that N.C. Public-Local Laws ch. 626 (1937), which prohibited the sale or giving away of beer and wine, violated N.C. Const. art. II, § 24(1)(j), and appellee restaurant was therefore entitled to a license to engage in the retail sale of beer and wine.


OVERVIEW: The restaurant sought a license to engage in the sale of beer and wine. The Board failed to issue a license on the ground that N.C. Public-Local Laws ch. 626 (1937) prohibited the issuance of a license. Except for the local act, the restaurant, upon compliance with the general laws regulating the sale of beer and wine, would have been free to pursue the retail sale of beer and wine as a trade. The trial court held that the local act violated N.C. Const. art. II, § 24(1)(j) because it regulated or governed a trade. On appeal, the Board suggested that since the local act also made it unlawful to give away the designated beverages, the local act placed a ban on a specified activity rather than regulating a trade in which the restaurant sought to engage. Affirming, the court held that the only substantial effect of the local act was to regulate the sale of alcoholic beverages, and its effect on trade was therefore not merely incidental. The court further held that the only practical consequence of the additional proscription against giving beverages away was most likely an aid to enforcement of the provisions regulating the sale of alcoholic beverages as a trade.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

1405 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


Britt v. Britt


No. 7510SC133


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


26 N.C. App. 132; 215 S.E.2d 172; 1975 N.C. App. LEXIS 1989


April 15, 1975, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
June 4, 1975, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by plaintiff from Godwin, Judge. Judgment entered 3 January 1975 in Superior Court, Wake County.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff wife filed an action in the Superior Court, Wake County (North Carolina) against defendants, husband and alleged creditors, and sought to set aside a foreclosure sale. The trial court granted defendants' motion for summary judgment, and the wife appealed.


OVERVIEW: The court found that there were three bases for the motion for summary judgment considered by the trial court: the filing of an unsigned affidavit of publication; the wife's allegations of fraud; and lack of notice of the foreclosure sale. The court affirmed the summary judgment for defendants. The court held that any objection that the wife might have had was cured by the filing of an affidavit, particularly in view of the fact that the wife did not dispute the fact that the notice was published. The court further found that the wife failed to allege in what respects she was induced to sign the deed of trust or upon what misrepresentations she relied. She only alleged that she signed the deed of trust at the request of her husband and did not allege to what extent he was indebted at the time. She also did not allege whether the other defendants had knowledge of the husband's intention to defraud his creditors and his wife. The court also held that there was no contention that notice of the sale was not given in accordance with the terms of the deed of trust or in accordance with the statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's grant of summary judgment to defendants in the wife's action to set aside a foreclosure sale.

 

1406 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


State v. Demott


No. 7418SC961


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


26 N.C. App. 14; 214 S.E.2d 781; 1975 N.C. App. LEXIS 1958


February 10, 1975, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
May 21, 1975, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendant from Crissman, Judge. Judgment entered 1 August 1974 in Superior Court, Guilford County.



DISPOSITION: In No. 74CR21709 -- No error. In No. 74CR21710 -- Judgment arrested. In No. 74CR21711 -- Judgment arrested.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged her three convictions for prostitution entered by the Guilford County Superior Court (North Carolina) upon a jury's verdict, on the grounds that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-204 was unconstitutional and that she had been entrapped.


OVERVIEW: Upon the request of a police agent, defendant gave the agent directions to her apartment. She met the agent at her apartment and offered to exchange sex for money. Defendant was charged with violating § 14-204(2), (4), and (6). On appeal, the court held that § 14-204 was not unconstitutional because none of the offenses were made an essential element of any other and a bilateral application of the facts required to prove any one of the charges would not necessarily haven proven either of the others. The court ruled that the evidence did not establish entrapment as a matter of law but that entrapment was a question for the jury. The court concluded, however, that defendant should not have been convicted of three separate violations under § 14-204 for her single act because the North Carolina Legislature's intent was not that defendant be separately and cumulatively punished under § 14-204 (2), (4), and (6). The Legislature's intent was to punish all who aid and abet prostitution by the various means set out in § 14-204, where a conviction would have otherwise been difficult to obtain, to the same extent as those who offer their bodies for sex were punished.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed one conviction and arrested judgment on the other two convictions.

 

1407 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


State v. Rook


No. 7510SC76


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


26 N.C. App. 33; 215 S.E.2d 159; 1975 N.C. App. LEXIS 1962


April 7, 1975, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
May 21, 1975, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendant from Bailey, Judge. Judgment entered 29 August 1974 in Superior Court, Wake County.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order of the Superior Court, Wake County (North Carolina), which denied his motion to quash an indictment against him for violation of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 20-106 after he was found in possession of a stolen car. Defendant argued that the language criminalizing possession of a car that the possessor had "reason to believe" was stolen denied due process because it was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: Police arrested defendant after he took the car on an errand and had mechanical difficulty. A passing officer recognized the car as stolen. On appeal, the court held that: (1) the challenged phrase defined the boundaries of what it condemned distinctly enough to provide an adequate warning, considering that there was a presumption that the statute was constitutional, the purpose of the statute was to discourage the possession of stolen vehicles by one who knew it was stolen or had reason to believe that it was stolen, and in constructing the statute it was a matter for the legislature to define and establish the degree of scienter upon which to rest the guilt of the accused; and (2) the evidence was sufficient to establish either knowledge or a belief by defendant that the vehicle he was driving was stolen where he and friends saw it at a mall the day before the arrest, one of the friends had been talking about stealing a car, and the next day that friend and another drove up in the car.


OUTCOME: The court held that there was no error on the part of the trial court, which properly denied the motion to quash the indictment.

1409 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


Myers v. Holshouser


No. 7510SC52


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


25 N.C. App. 683; 214 S.E.2d 630; 1975 N.C. App. LEXIS 2379


March 20, 1975, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
May 21, 1975, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by respondents from Bailey, Judge. Judgment entered 20 November 1974 in Superior Court, Wake County.



DISPOSITION: Reversed in part. Affirmed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner liquor sales representative appealed a decision by the Superior Court, Wake County (North Carolina), which held that respondent North Carolina Board of Alcoholic Control could require petitioner to produce business books and records relating to his liquor business without abridging his constitutional rights under the Fourth Amendment.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner liquor company representative brought an action against respondent North Carolina Board of Alcoholic Control for a declaratory judgment that he was entitled to renewal of his liquor license and an injunction directing respondent to issue it after respondent denied a license upon his refusal to open his books to inspection. The court reversed an order directing respondent to issue the permit. It held that the trial court erred in granting injunctive relief because respondent's statutory power of investigation did not abridge petitioner's rights under the Fourth Amendment. The court found that because respondent's statutory investigative authority was not unconstitutional, the trial court's authority was limited to a declaration that petitioner was entitled to a due process hearing before respondent, upon its order to produce records, before action was taken to revoke or deny renewal of petitioner's permit. The court affirmed the declaratory judgment that respondent deprived petitioner of a property right without due process in that respondent took the permit from him by the refusal to renew it without notice or a hearing, as required by N.C. Gen. Stat. § 18A-15(12).


OUTCOME: The court reversed that part of the trial court's decision directing respondent North Carolina Board of Alcoholic Control to issue a liquor license to petitioner liquor sales representative because respondent had the authority to investigate his books and its investigation was not unconstitutional. The court affirmed the holding that respondent exceeded its authority in refusing to renew the license without notice and a chance to be heard.

1411 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


William R. Andrews Associates v. Sodibar Systems of D.C., Inc.


No. 7415DC934


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


25 N.C. App. 372; 213 S.E.2d 411; 1975 N.C. App. LEXIS 2266


January 22, 1975, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
April 16, 1975, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendant from Allen, Judge. Judgment entered 30 July 1974 in District Court, Orange County.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant foreign corporation appealed from the order of the Civil Session of Orange County District Court (North Carolina) denying its motion to quash service of process and to dismiss a contract action brought by plaintiff business association for lack of personal jurisdiction pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1-75.4(5)d.


OVERVIEW: The corporation was incorporated in Delaware and sold soda fountain supplies. A representative of the business association visited the corporation at its principal place of business in Washington, D.C. The corporation sold the business used steel canisters and bought new aluminum canisters from it. The aluminum canisters were shipped to the corporation from North Carolina. As instructed, the corporation shipped the steel canisters to a customer in Florida. Later, the business discovered that the steel canisters were not usable, and it filed a contract action for breach of warranty against the corporation in North Carolina. The corporation filed a motion to quash service of process and to dismiss the action for lack of personal jurisdiction. The evidence provided showed that the corporation was not domesticated in North Carolina, did no business there, and solicited no business there. The trial court denied the motion. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment. The court noted that the dispute only related to the shipment from Washington D.C. to Florida. Thus, the court held that the case did not fall within the North Carolina long-arm statute, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1-75.4(5)d.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order denying the foreign corporation's motion to quash and to dismiss the business association's contract action for lack of personal jurisdiction.

 

1412 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


Leak v. High Point City Council


No. 7418SC1094


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


25 N.C. App. 394; 213 S.E.2d 386; 1975 N.C. App. LEXIS 2271


March 11, 1975, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
April 16, 1975, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendants from Rousseau, Judge. Judgment entered 3 October 1974, Superior Court, Guilford County.



DISPOSITION: Vacated in part and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a city council and its members, sought review of an order from the Superior Court (North Carolina), which determined certain issues regarding the press coverage of investigative hearings and the city council's' rights under N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 160A-80, 160A-81. The claimants filed a motion to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: The court found that the case involved N.C. Gen. Stat. § 160A-80 and N.C. Gen. Stat. § 160A-81 and that the determination of the issues involved was of public interest. Therefore, the court declined to dismiss the appeal as moot. The court confined the issue before it to the authority of a city council in conducting investigative hearings to adopt rules providing not only for press but also for radio and television coverage. The court held that where a legislature made a proper delegation of power to a municipality, a court could not interfere with exercise of that power unless manifest abuse was shown. The court found that there had been no showing by the claimants that radio and television coverage would disrupt the hearings, would violate any constitutional rights of the claimants, or would in any other way constitute a manifest abuse of this discretionary power delegated to the city council and its members.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the part of the order of the trial court that entered a preliminary injunction that prohibited live radio or television coverage of the hearings and the presence of voice or television recording devices in the hearing room and remanded the cause.

Duggins v. North Carolina State Board of Certified Public Accountant Examiners


No. 7410SC971


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


25 N.C. App. 131; 212 S.E.2d 657; 1975 N.C. App. LEXIS 2202


February 11, 1975, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
March 19, 1975, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendant from Bailey, Judge. Judgment entered 7 August 1974 in Superior Court, Wake County.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant board challenged the judgment of the Wake County Court (North Carolina), which held that plaintiff lawyer had satisfied the experience requirement of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 93-12.


OVERVIEW: The lawyer was also an accountant and argued that he had experience as a public accountant. The court reversed the judgment and held that the plain meaning of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 93-12(5) was that an applicant for licensing as a certified public accountant was to have two years experience on the field staff of a certified public accountant in the public practice of accountancy. An administrative interpretation of a statute, acquiesced in over a long period of time, was properly considered in the construction of § 93-12(5). The court found that experience with an attorney-certified public accountant (CPA) was not sufficient for certification unless the attorney-CPA was in the public practice of accountancy as that phrase was defined by N.C. Gen. Stat. § 93-12. Further, a lawyer could not ethically hold himself out to the public as an accountant. The court held that no discrimination was shown. The lawyer failed to show that the classification of which he complained was essentially arbitrary and without any reasonable basis.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in favor of the lawyer. The trial court erred in concluding that the lawyer had the experience required for state qualification as an accountant.

 

1414 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


State v. Jarrell


No. 7426SC811


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


24 N.C. App. 610; 211 S.E.2d 837; 1975 N.C. App. LEXIS 2452


November 14, 1974, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
February 19, 1975, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendants from Falls, Judge. Judgments entered 3 April 1974 in Superior Court, Mecklenburg County.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed a decision of the Mecklenburg County Superior Court (North Carolina), which convicted them for performing sexual acts in violation of N. C. Gen. Stat. § 14-177. Defendants asserted that certain evidence was unlawfully admitted at trial, that evidence which was taken in a search that was unlawful under the Fourth Amendment ought to have been suppressed, and that their motion for a mistrial ought to have been granted.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted for having committed proscribed sexual acts in a public bathroom in violation of N. C. Gen. Stat. § 14-177 and the court affirmed their conviction. In light of the overwhelming evidence of their guilt, the trial court's admission of certain disputed evidence did not require reversal even if this violated their constitutional rights. Defendants asserted that photographs taken of them in the bathroom constituted an illegal search which violated their Fourth Amendment rights and as such claimed that this material ought to have been suppressed at trial. The court rejected this argument, finding that the reasonable expectation to privacy granted by the Fourth Amendment was only in effect if the accused exhibited an actual expectation of privacy and that expectation was one which society was prepared to accept as reasonable. No such expectation of privacy extended to public bathrooms. The court found that no constitutional restrictions prevented the state from punishing sexual acts in a public area. Finally, the court found no abuse of discretion in the trial court's denial of defendants' motions for mistrial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendants' conviction.

 

1415 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


State v. Brooks


No. 7416SC96


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


24 N.C. App. 338; 210 S.E.2d 535; 1975 N.C. App. LEXIS 2386



January 2, 1975, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendant from Bailey, Judge, 9 July 1973 Session of Superior Court held in Robeson County.

Defendant was charged by warrants with (1) inciting a riot, (2) engaging in a riot, and (3) failing to comply with a lawful command to disperse, violations respectively of G.S. 14-288.2(d), G.S. 14-288.2(b), and G.S. 14-288.5(b). He was convicted in the District Court and appealed to the Superior Court, where he again pled not guilty to all charges.

The State's evidence tended to show the following events. On the afternoon of 23 March 1973 defendant, who was sometimes referred to by the witnesses as "Chief" Brooks, addressed a gathering in Pembroke of fifty to sixty people, most of whom were Indians. He announced the scheduling of an "organization meeting" for 6:45 p.m. at the Prospect School and reported that the County School Superintendent had indicated that the school would not be available for the meeting because of construction taking place on the premises. Defendant told the gathering he did not think this was a "reasonable or prudent" excuse for not allowing them to use the facility as a meeting place and that he wanted the people of Robeson County to know "that [***2] he was willing to die at the steps of the school and that if there were any riot clad armed law enforcement officers on the school grounds that they were trespassing on Indian property" and he hoped "they know what that means."

The Prospect School is on the south side of a State paved road across from the Prospect Methodist Church, which is on the north side of the road. The paved portion of the road is 20 feet wide and the highway right-of-way extends 30 feet on either side of its center line. Shortly before dark on 23 March 1973 a crowd, composed mostly of Indians, began to assemble on the church grounds. Approximately 40 to 45 law enforcement officers, including Highway Patrolmen, Sheriff's Deputies, and SBI Agents, were stationed on the school grounds under the command of Deputy Sheriff Hubert Stone. By 6:30 p.m. some 75 to 100 people had assembled on the north side of the road across from the school, and this crowd continued to grow during the night, reaching its maximum of from 175 to 200 people sometime between 11:00 p.m. and midnight. Throughout the evening defendant was present, clad in a blanket draped around his shoulders, and from time to time he was seen addressing [***3] the crowd from the church steps. The officers could not hear what defendant said, but when he spoke members of the crowd would yell, "Red Power," and "stick their arms up in the air and holler real loud and then he spoke some more."

As the evening progressed the crowd became increasingly noisy and boisterous. A bonfire was built on the church grounds and there was considerable shouting, dancing, and singing of "We Shall Overcome." Traffic on the road was heavy, with much coming and going. Cases of beer were brought, and some members of the crowd were drinking. The crowd began to shout insults and threats towards the officers stationed across the road at times threatening to come across the road and "beat the officers to death." Passing motorists were stopped, and some were dragged from their cars while members of the crowd beat on the hoods, sides, and trunks of the cars. Persons in the crowd began to throw beer and other drink bottles at the officers, and the officers observed that some in the crowd were armed with knives and sticks. From time to time a knot of people would form and start across the road toward the officers. When this happened, defendant would call them back,  [***4] telling them not to cross the road until he made his decision and that when he made his decision, they were going across to the school grounds. Defendant also announced to the officers across the road that if any of "his people" were injured, he would "declare war on Robeson County." A pistol shot and a shotgun blast were heard, and the verbal insults and threats toward the officers became increasingly belligerent and violent in tone. One patrolman described the situation of the officers as one in which they "could very easily get killed and never know who did it."

Shortly after midnight a bottle was thrown out of the crowd, striking the pavement and splintering glass on the officers. Just then three blasts were fired in quick succession from a shotgun, and the officers heard the pellets falling on their parked vehicles. When this occurred, Deputy Sheriff Stone, after having been advised to do so by other officers present, gave the command to the crowd to disperse. Using a bullhorn, he told the crowd it had five minutes in which to leave. This directive was greeted by an increased flurry of bottle throwing from the crowd. However, approximately twenty-five people did obey the [***5] command to disperse and left the area. Others, including defendant Brooks, remained. After waiting six minutes, Deputy Sheriff Stone ordered his officers to cross the road and to arrest those who had refused to comply with his command to disperse. The officers then crossed the road and began arresting persons in the crowd who had refused to leave. In all, forty-eight men, including defendant Brooks, six women, and one boy were arrested. Others in the crowd evaded arrest by fleeing into the darkness.

Defendant did not offer any evidence. He was found guilty on all charges, and from judgments imposing concurrent jail sentences, he appealed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Criminal Session of Robeson County Superior Court (North Carolina), which convicted defendant of inciting a riot, engaging in a riot, and failing to comply with a lawful command to disperse, violations of N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 14-288.2, 14-288.2(b), and 14-288.5(b).


OVERVIEW: Defendant addressed a crowd of people, most of whom were Native Americans, and stated that he wanted to hold an organizational meeting at a school on the land of the reservation, but that the county would not allow them to use the building. He told the crowd that he was willing to die at the steps of the school. That night a crowd gathered on church property across the street from the school, while 40-50 officers were stationed across the street. The crowd grew to approximately 200 people. As the evening went on, they became more noisy and boisterous. Defendant stated that if the officers came across the street he would declare war. The crowd was asked to leave after gunshots where allegedly fired from the crowd at the officers' cars, but defendant and others refused and were arrested. Defendant asserted that the statutes under which he was convicted were unconstitutional in that they were overly broad and infringed on his constitutional rights under the First Amendment. The court found nothing constitutionally offensive in the statutes, but determined that the trial court erred in its jury instructions regarding defendant's failure to comply with the officer's order to disperse.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the charges of inciting a riot and engaging in a riot. The court reversed defendant's conviction of failing to comply with a lawful order to disperse and remanded that for a new trial.

In re Smith


No. 7412DC554


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


24 N.C. App. 321; 210 S.E.2d 453; 1974 N.C. App. LEXIS 1994


September 17, 1974, Argued in the Court of Appeals  
December 18, 1974, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by the juvenile from Carter, District Court Judge, 2 May 1974 Session of District Court held in Cumberland County.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a juvenile, sought review from the order of the Cumberland County District Court (North Carolina), which transferred him to a superior court. Defendant was charged with rape, a violation of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-280.


OVERVIEW: Defendant alleged that § 7A-280 was unconstitutional. The court held § 7A-280 to be constitutional. Defendant also alleged that the trial court was required to conduct a separate evidentiary hearing on the cause for transfer. The court rejected the argument that § 7A-280 mandated a separate hearing. Defendant's disposition order specified the reason for the transfer to be that if defendant was found guilty of the charge of rape, the North Carolina Board of Youth Development was not in position to render appropriate custodial rehabilitative services. This was a minimal specification of reason under § 7A-280. However, in exercising its discretion to transfer the action, the trial court found that the transfer best served the needs of defendant. Finally, there was no claim or contention that counsel for defendant was denied the right to examine any court or probation records considered by the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order.

 

1417 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


Jolliff v. Winslow


No. 741DC668


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


24 N.C. App. 107; 210 S.E.2d 221; 1974 N.C. App. LEXIS 1941


November 14, 1974, Argued before the Court of Appeals  
December 4, 1974, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendant from Horner, District Court Judge, 8 April 1974 Session of District Court held in Perquimans County.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant individuals challenged the judgment from the District Court, Perquimans County (North Carolina), which issued a temporary restraining order and preliminary injunction in favor of plaintiff individual.


OVERVIEW: The individuals advanced arguments that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1A-1, N.C. R. Civ. P. 65(b) violated the equal protection and due process clauses of the North Carolina Constitution and the United States Constitution, and that the authorization of a preliminary injunction was likewise unconstitutional. The court affirmed the judgment and held that § 1A-1, R. 65(b) permitted the issuance of temporary restraining orders without notice to the adverse party when it appeared that damage would result before notice could be served and a hearing held. The court found that procedural protections were set forth in Rule 65 that were designed to check the extent of use of the remedy. The court found nothing unconstitutional about a rule that permitted the issuance of a temporary restraining order. Under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1A-1, N.C. R. Civ. P. 65, a preliminary injunction, unlike a temporary restraining order, required notice to the adverse party and a hearing. The court found that a preliminary mandatory injunction could be issued when an easement into one's property had been obstructed and that the individuals' second argument was without merit.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that issued a temporary restraining order and preliminary injunction in favor of the individual. The court held that neither a temporary restraining order nor preliminary injunctions were unconstitutional.

 

1418 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


In re Simmons


No. 7413DC387


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


24 N.C. App. 28; 210 S.E.2d 84; 1974 N.C. App. LEXIS 1921



November 25, 1974, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by respondent from Walton, District Judge, 13 December 1973 Session of District Court held in Brunswick County.

This juvenile proceeding was commenced by a petition signed by Julie and David Robinson in which petitioners alleged that respondent, a 14-year-old boy, was a delinquent child as defined by G.S. 7A-278(2) in that "the child did unlawfully use in telephonic communications with Julie Robinson certain words and language which were profane, vulgar, lewd, lascivious, indecent in nature and connotation . . . constituting harassment by telephone" in violation of G.S. 14-196(a) (1). The petition set forth the particular words respondent was charged with having used and the dates the telephone calls were alleged to have been made. After a hearing at which respondent was represented by counsel and his parents were present, the court entered an order finding as a fact that respondent "did unlawfully call by telephonic communications Mrs. Julie Robinson and did use words of a profane, vulgar and lewd nature in violation of G.S. 14-196(a) (1)." On this finding, the court concluded that respondent was a delinquent child, and entered judgment placing respondent on probation [***2] for a period of two years upon certain conditions. Respondent appealed.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent minor appealed a judgment of the district court (North Carolina), which found that the minor was a delinquent child, in a juvenile proceeding commenced by petitioners, wife and husband.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners alleged that the minor was a delinquent child as defined by N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-278(2) in that the minor unlawfully used in telephonic communications with the wife certain words and language which constituted harassment by telephone in violation of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-196(a)(1). The minor filed motions contending that § 14-196(a)(1) was unconstitutional on the grounds that it was vague and overbroad and violated the First and Fourteenth Amendments. The district court denied the motions and found that the minor violated § 14-196(a)(1). The court affirmed. The court held that while the language of the statute was broad, it was neither so vague as to be easily misunderstood nor so broad as to reach beyond the state's power to enact. The court held further that use of one's telephone clearly involved substantial privacy interests which the state could recognize and protect. The court held that § 14-196(a)(1) sought to protect that interest from an invasion made in an essentially intolerable manner and that the means chosen were both appropriate and sufficiently narrow to achieve the legitimate ends sought to be attained.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the district court's judgment, which found that the minor violated the statute prohibiting harassment by telephone.

 

1419 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


Redevelopment Com. of Greenville v. Unco, Inc.


No. 733SC794


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


23 N.C. App. 574; 209 S.E.2d 841; 1974 N.C. App. LEXIS 2162



November 20, 1974, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by respondents from judgment dated 10 May 1973 entered by Tillery, Judge, after hearing at the 26 February 1973 Session of Superior Court held in Pitt County.

Special proceeding to condemn land for an urban renewal project. Prior to 1966 the Greenville City Council approved the Shore Drive Redevelopment Project, an urban renewal plan formulated by the City's Redevelopment Commission and certified by the City's Planning and Zoning Commission under the provisions of the North Carolina Urban Redevelopment Law, G.S. 160-454 et seq. (now G.S. 160A-500 et seq.). The plan designated as a "blighted area" a portion of downtown Greenville bounded on the north by Tar River and on the south by Second Street. No question is raised on this appeal concerning any land in the project as originally approved.

On 28 December 1966 the City Council adopted an amended plan which added certain areas to the project. One of these, identified as Parcel 13, contains the property which is the subject matter of the present litigation. Parcel 13 consists of approximately the northern half of a city block and is bounded on the north by Second Street, on the south by Courthouse Lane, on the east [***2] by Evans Street, and on the west by Washington Street. The southern half of the block is occupied by the Pitt County Courthouse. On 28 December 1966 Parcel 13 contained six structures: A building formerly used as a Catholic Church, the National Guard Armory, the Edwards Building, the Dudley house, the Buck house, and the building owned by respondent Unco, Incorporated, which is the subject of this litigation. In 1966 Pitt County held title to the Edwards Building and the Buck house and, with the City of Greenville, the Armory (subject to the rights of the State of North Carolina); the other three structures were privately owned. Unco's property, a lot fronting 39 feet on the north side of Courthouse Lane across from the Courthouse and having a depth of 85 feet, contained a small frame house which had been converted in 1957 into an office building which was leased to and occupied by respondent Sam B. Underwood as his law office.

Prior to adoption of the amended plan, the Redevelopment Commission and Pitt County entered into an agreement dated 24 September 1965 whereby, among other things, the Redevelopment Commission agreed to convey, if acquired, Parcel 13 to the County. This [***3] agreement was subsequently incorporated into a second agreement dated 16 February 1967 between the Commission, the City of Greenville, and the County, whereby the Commission agreed to acquire and clear all of Parcel 13 and to resell the same to Pitt County, and the County agreed to use the property in accordance with plans and specifications conforming with the urban renewal plan for centralizing the County offices and for parking.

After an unsuccessful effort to purchase the Unco property, the Revedelopment Commission commenced the present proceeding on 31 July 1969 by filing a petition for condemnation directed solely to the Unco property. (The Commission subsequently acquired by purchase and conveyed to the County all other properties in Parcel 13.) Respondents filed answer in which they denied the Redevelopment Commission's right to obtain the property by eminent domain, alleging that the inclusion of the Unco property in the 1966 amendment to the urban renewal plan was unconstitutionally arbitrary and capricious, and that, by virtue of the 1965 and 1967 agreements between the Commission and Pitt County, the County was attempting to exercise indirectly a legislatively unauthorized [***4] power of eminent domain.

The Clerk appointed Commissioners, who valued the Unco property at $ 39,600.00, and by order dated 8 April 1971 the Clerk confirmed the Commissioners' award. Upon appeal to the Superior Court, the matter was heard de novo by the Judge upon stipulations entered into by the parties and upon evidence presented. Among other matters, the parties stipulated that the fair market value of the Unco property was $ 39,600.00, which sum the Redevelopment Commission paid into court, and it was further stipulated that the Commission had adequate funds on hand to complete the Shore Drive Redevelopment Project. Following the hearing, the Judge entered judgment making findings of fact, conclusions of law, and adjudging that the Redevelopment Commission was entitled to acquire and did acquire title to the Unco property by eminent domain and awarding respondents $ 39,600.00 with interest from the date of taking. Respondents Unco, Incorporated, and Sam B. Underwood and wife, appealed.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a office building owner, its lessee and the lessee's wife, sought review of a decision of the Pitt County Superior Court (North Carolina), which determined that appellee, the Redevelopment Commission of the City of Greenville, was entitled to acquire and did acquire the owner's property, in a condemnation petition by the Commission to acquire the property for an urban renewal project.


OVERVIEW: An urban renewal project proposed by the Commission pursuant to the North Carolina Urban Redevelopment Law, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 160-454 et seq., was approved. Later, the city council adopted an amended plan that added certain areas to the project, including a parcel that contained public property and the owner's office building. In a condemnation suit, appellants challenged the trial court's determination that the Commission acquired title to the building by eminent domain. The court noted, inter alia, that the evidence supported the trial court's finding that the parcel was a blighted area under the Redevelopment Law. Further, there was no error in the trial court's finding that the Commission and the city council did not act arbitrarily or capriciously in including the parcel in the urban renewal plan. Appellants' claim that the city, the commission, and the county wrongfully cooperated to use the urban renewal processes to accomplish a result primarily of benefit to the county that could not have otherwise have been achieved was beside the point where such cooperation was statutorily authorized and directed and where the stated purposes of urban renewal were also accomplished.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

State v. Mills


No. 749SC742


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


23 N.C. App. 485; 209 S.E.2d 370; 1974 N.C. App. LEXIS 2125


October 14, 1974, Argued in the Court of Appeals  
November 6, 1974, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by Winstead from Bailey, Judge, 29 April 1974 Criminal Session of Franklin County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, defendant's surety on his appearance bond, challenged the judgment of the Franklin County Superior Court (North Carolina), which held that the surety became indebted to the State based on defendant's failure to appear in court.


OVERVIEW: After a local radio station announced that a special session of criminal court would convene and a court calendar was prepared with defendant's case set, defendant failed to appear in court. The trial court entered a judgment holding the surety liable for defendant's failure to appear pursuant to an appearance bond, and the court affirmed. The court held that the surety's argument that he was not under a duty to secure the appearance of defendant at a special session of court of which he had no notice, as opposed to a regular court session, was without merit. The appearance bond was broad enough to cover special sessions, and when defendant failed to appear, the surety became indebted to the State. The trial court acted within its discretion in reducing the forfeiture amount under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15-116, and there was no error of law on the face of the record.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

State v. Crabtree


No. 7414SC699


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


23 N.C. App. 491; 209 S.E.2d 299; 1974 N.C. App. LEXIS 2127


September 24, 1974, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
November 6, 1974, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendant from Chess, Special Judge, 2 May 1974 Session of Superior Court held in Durham County.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order of the Durham County Superior Court (North Carolina), which convicted him of failing to use due care while driving, in violation of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 20-141(c). The trial court overruled defendant's motion to quash the warrant and denied his motion in arrest of judgment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that § 20-141(c) required conduct in terms so vague that men of common intelligence must necessarily guess at its meaning and differ as to its application. He maintained that the statute was too indefinite to be constitutionally valid. On appeal, the court held that the statute was not unconstitutionally vague, in light of the fact that (1) the § 20-141(c) standard of "due care" was more definite than a "due caution and circumspection" standard found in a reckless driving statute, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 20-140(b), which statute previously had been found constitutional; and (2) a different subsection of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 20-141 had been upheld as constitutional in a prior case. The court found defendant's argument unpersuasive that the trial court erred in the jury charge to such an extent that the language used failed to apply the law to the facts of the case as required by N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1-180.


OUTCOME: The court found no error in the conviction.

State v. Joyner


No. 7421SC704


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


23 N.C. App. 27; 208 S.E.2d 233; 1974 N.C. App. LEXIS 2002


August 28, 1974, Heard in Court of Appeals  
September 18, 1974, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendant from McConnell, Judge, 22 April 1974 Session of Superior Court held in Forsyth County.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his conviction in the Forsyth County Superior Court (North Carolina) for violation of a city's zoning ordinance regarding salvage yards.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was accused in a warrant of violating the city's zoning ordinance that required the removal of defendant's salvage yard within three years of the effective date of the ordinance. Defendant claimed that the ordinance was unconstitutional and that the trial court erred in overruling his motion to quash the warrant and motion for nonsuit. The court found no error in defendant's conviction. The court held that the warrant stated the time that defendant operated his salvage business after the effective date of the ordinance, that the ordinance's provisions for the removal of salvage yards within three years was not arbitrary, unreasonable, or confiscatory, that the ordinance was constitutional, and that the trial court properly overruled defendant's motion to quash the warrant. The court also held that defendant made no attempt to comply with the ordinance, that defendant could have made a less costly move during the three-year grace period, and that the trial court properly denied defendant's motion for nonsuit.


OUTCOME: The court found no error in defendant's conviction for violation of the city's zoning ordinance.

 

1425 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


State v. McAuliffe


No. 7430SC367


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


22 N.C. App. 601; 207 S.E.2d 1; 1974 N.C. App. LEXIS 2392



August 7, 1974, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendant from Copeland, Judge, 3 December 1973 Session of Jackson County Superior Court.

The defendant was charged in a bill of indictment with the possession of marijuana with the intent to distribute in violation of G.S. 90-95. The defendant's wife was also charged with the same offense. The cases were consolidated for trial, and pleas of not guilty were entered by each defendant. A judgment as of nonsuit was entered as to the charges against the defendant's wife; the defendant was found guilty as charged. From the imposition of a five year active sentence, the defendant gave notice of appeal.

Special agent James T. Maxey of the State Bureau of Investigation testified that he had occasion to go to the residence of Norman Allen West at approximately 2:40 a.m., on 1 September 1973. Agent Maxey was accompanied by several other law enforcement officers. The defendant's residence was located at the Davis Trailer Park. Upon his arrival, he knocked on the front door of the trailer.

Agent Maxey had a conversation with the defendant upon his arrival. He warned the defendant of his constitutional rights and asked him if a 1967 blue Mustang in the driveway belonged [***2] to the defendant. The defendant did not answer; whereupon Agent Maxey informed the defendant that he had a search warrant to search the automobile and asked again if it belonged to the defendant. The defendant stated that it was his and gave a key to the trunk of the automobile to Officer Maxey.

A large aluminum suitcase was discovered in the trunk of the automobile. The suitcase contained twelve bricks of a greenish colored vegetable material. One of the bricks was analyzed and found to contain 721.6 grams of marijuana.

The defendant offered no evidence.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were charged in the Jackson County Superior Court (North Carolina) with possession of marijuana with intent to distribute in violation of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 90-95. In consolidated cases, pleas of not guilty were entered, a judgment of nonsuit was entered as to one defendant, and the other defendant was found guilty as charged, which he appealed.


OVERVIEW: A search warrant for defendant's car was obtained based on information from a confidential informer. He admitted ownership of the car, and a suitcase containing approximately ten thousand grams of marijuana was found therein. On appeal, the court held that the presumption in § 90-95 that possession of more than five grams of marijuana created a presumption that possession was for the purpose of distribution was constitutional. The presumption was not conclusive and it did not shift the burden of proof from the State. The trial court was not required to disclose the identity of the informer because there was no indication that he was a participant in the crime, all evidence necessary to convict was from the direct observation of police officers, and defendant did not offer evidence of entrapment or other defenses. The fact that the trial judge had a whispered conversation with a witness about defendant's drug use did not indicate that the trial judge was biased because the trial judge was attempting to determine if the disclosure of the drug use would have aided the defense. The trial court clearly gave defendant the opportunity to present evidence, and he refused.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

 

1426 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


State v. Orange


No. 741SC404


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


22 N.C. App. 220; 206 S.E.2d 377; 1974 N.C. App. LEXIS 2285



July 3, 1974, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Certiorari to review defendant's trial before Copeland, Judge, 10 September 1973 Session of Superior Court held in Chowan County.

Defendant was charged in a warrant with unlawfully and willfully failing and refusing to disperse when commanded to do so by Sheriff Troy Toppin, a law enforcement officer responsible for keeping the peace, when said officer reasonably believed that disorderly conduct was occurring by the assemblage of three or more persons, a misdemeanor violation under G.S. 14-288.5. After trial and conviction in the district court, defendant appealed to the superior court, where he again pled not guilty. The State offered evidence tending to show: On the morning of 5 June 1973, approximately 30 Negroes were arrested in connection with a protest in Chowan County. They were taken to the "auditorium or seating area" of the Chowan County courthouse, in Edenton, N. C., for processing. While the arrested persons were still inside, approximately 30 or 35 additional Negroes approached the courthouse chanting "we want to get in the courthouse, we want to get in." Defendant, a former professional football player who was 30 years old, weighed 368 pounds, and was [***2] six feet three inches tall, was at the front of the crowd. When the crowd, which covered the entire front portion of the courthouse, reached the bottom of the three steps leading up to the front door, their path was blocked by several police officers. The members of the crowd, including the defendant, pushed against the riot sticks of the officers. Several times, Chowan County Sheriff Troy Toppin asked the group to disperse. Eight or ten persons obeyed, but twenty-one remained, chanting "we want to be arrested, go ahead and arrest us"; and "why can't we come in, we want to come in." These persons, including the defendant, who had remained at the front of the group, were then arrested. Although during the commotion it appears that defendant at one time slipped on the courthouse steps and fell to his knees, he received no injury, and the confrontation ended essentially without violence. The defendant testified to a similar version of events, although he stated that he had never pushed against the officers stationed at the courthouse door. He offered evidence to show that the group was seeking entry into the courthouse in order to protest and to investigate accusations of police [***3] brutality towards the previously-arrested Negroes.

The jury found defendant guilty as charged, and judgment was entered imposing an active six-month prison sentence. Defendant appealed. To permit perfection of the appeal, this Court subsequently issued writ of certiorari.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment the Superior Court, Chowan County (North Carolina), which convicted him of unlawfully and willfully failing and refusing to disperse when commanded to do so by a law enforcement officer responsible for keeping the peace, when said officer reasonably believed that disorderly conduct was occurring by the assemblage of three or more persons, a misdemeanor violation under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-288.5.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, who participated in a courthouse protest, was charged with violating § 14-288.5. He was convicted by the district court. On appeal, the superior court convicted defendant upon the jury's verdict. The court issued a writ of certiorari but found no error. The court held that § 14-288.4(a)(2), which provided a definition of disorderly conduct, was not unconstitutionally vague under the First Amendment, finding that the legislature only intended to prohibit "fighting words." Defendant's motion to be declared indigent and to be provided with a free transcript of the district court proceedings was properly denied because it was not a timely request. His motion for nonsuit was properly denied because sufficient evidence supported a jury finding that a law enforcement officer responsible for keeping the peace, had reasonable grounds to believe that disorderly conduct by an assemblage of three or more persons was occurring at the time he issued the command to disperse. In addition, the command was given in a manner reasonably calculated to be communicated to the assemblage of which defendant was a part and defendant willfully refused to obey the command.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the superior court's judgment that convicted defendant of unlawfully and willfully failing and refusing to disperse when commanded to do so by a law enforcement officer responsible for keeping the peace, when said officer reasonably believed that disorderly conduct was occurring by the assemblage of three or more persons.

In re Bullard


No. 7412DC487


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


22 N.C. App. 245; 206 S.E.2d 305; 1974 N.C. App. LEXIS 2291


May 29, 1974, Heard in Court of Appeals  
July 3, 1974, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by juveniles from Dupree, Judge, 11 and 18 January 1974 Sessions of District Court held in Hoke County.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant juveniles appealed from an order of the Hoke County District Court, Juvenile Division (North Carolina) transferring their cases to a Superior Court.


OVERVIEW: The District Court found that there was probable cause that defendants committed the crimes charged and that the needs of the juveniles and the best interest of the State were best served by a transfer to the Superior Court for trial. On appeal, the court held that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-280 was not unconstitutionally vague because it was not a penal statute, so it did not place anyone in the position of being unable to determine whether his or her conduct was against the law. The form of the District Court's order was proper because the North Carolina statutes relating to juveniles did not require that a determination of probable cause to be supported by detailed findings of fact. Defendants' double jeopardy rights were not violated. Although the orders incorrectly stated that a dispositional hearing had been conducted, there had been only a determination of probable cause, and not an adjudication of guilt, so defendants had not been placed in jeopardy or punished.


OUTCOME: The transfer orders of the District Court were affirmed.

Taylor v. Raleigh


No. 7410SC106


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


22 N.C. App. 259; 206 S.E.2d 401; 1974 N.C. App. LEXIS 2294



July 3, 1974, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Hobgood, Judge, 13 August 1973 Session of Wake County Superior Court.

On 21 December 1970, the City Council of Raleigh unanimously adopted zoning Ordinance No. (1970) 33-ZC-91. This ordinance rezoned approximately 39 acres located outside the corporate limits of Raleigh from an R-4 classification to an R-6 classification. The former classification does not allow multifamily housing, but the latter does.

On 6 January 1972, the defendant Mangum filed a petition with the City of Raleigh to annex approximately 39 acres under the authority of Chapter 989 of the 1967 Session Laws of North Carolina. This is the same land which had previously been rezoned to the R-6 classification. After giving the required public notice and holding the required public hearings, on 20 March 1972, the City Council unanimously adopted Ordinance No. (1972) 211 providing for noncontiguous annexation of the 39 acre tract in question.

On 2 January 1973, the City Council adopted resolutions providing for the acquisition by condemnation of certain portions of the plaintiffs' land on which to construct a sewage line between the city limit of Raleigh and the noncontiguous 39 acres previously [***2] annexed. Special proceedings actions were subsequently instituted to condemn the needed property.

On 12 January 1973, the plaintiffs filed an action seeking to declare unconstitutional both the rezoning ordinance enacted in 1970 and the annexation ordinance enacted in 1972. In addition, the action sought to restrain the defendant City of Raleigh from condemning the land of the plaintiffs for the construction of the sewage line. Each party stipulated to the factual situation as outlined, and each party moved for summary judgment. Summary judgment was granted in favor of defendants, and plaintiffs gave notice of appeal.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowner appealed a judgment of the Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina), which granted summary judgment in favor of defendant City in an action to declare a rezoning ordinance and an annexation ordinance to be unconstitutional, and to restrain the City from condemning the landowner's property.


OVERVIEW: In 1970, the City rezoned certain land located outside the city's corporate limits. In 1972, after holding public hearings, the City annexed the land. The City then sought to acquire through condemnation, certain portions of the landowners' property on which to construct a sewer line. The landowners brought an action to declare the rezoning and the annexation ordinances unconstitutional, and to restrain the City from condemning the landowners' property. The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment in favor of the City. The court held that the landowners did not have standing to contest the action more than two years after the zoning ordinance was enacted. Under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1-254, a declaratory judgment was the proper method to determine its validity. The landowners never sought to challenge the ordinance by seeking a declaratory judgment. Because the landowners had not been diligent in asserting their rights in opposition to the zoning change, the court held that they were barred by the doctrine of laches from asserting such rights. The court also held that the landowners lacked standing because their property was outside the annexed area.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

1429 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


Richmond Food Stores, Inc. v. Jones


No. 7410SC391


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


22 N.C. App. 272; 206 S.E.2d 346; 1974 N.C. App. LEXIS 2298



July 3, 1974, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendant from Hobgood, Judge, 11 December 1973 Session of Superior Court held in Wake County.

Plaintiff seeks to recover certain taxes which it had paid under protest, contending that their levy constituted a discriminatory and undue burden on interstate commerce in violation of Article I, Section 8 of the Constitution of the United States.

The taxes were collected under G.S. 105-113.45, which, among other things, levies a one cent tax on each bottled soft drink distributed in the State. Payment of the tax is required under G.S. 105-113.51 to be evidenced by the affixing of tax paid crowns or stamps.

Plaintiff is a nonresident distributor and does not have a commercial domicile in the State. Plaintiff distributes bottled soft drinks in this State.

G.S. 105-113.56A, in pertinent part, is as follows:

"Alternate method of payment of tax. -- Instead of paying the tax levied in this Article in the manner otherwise provided, any resident distributor or wholesale dealer, and any distributor or wholesale dealer having a commercial domicile in this State may pay the tax in the following manner, with respect to bottled soft drinks:

Beginning with sales made on and [***2] after October 1, 1969, of bottled soft drinks subject to the tax, sales reports shall be made to the Commissioner on or before the fifteenth day of each succeeding month, accompanied by payment of the tax due, determined as follows: For the first fifteen thousand gross of bottled soft drinks sold annually, seventy-two cents (72 cents) per gross; for all in excess of fifteen thousand gross, one cent (1 cent) per bottle. In addition, there shall be allowed a discount of eight percent (8%) of the said tax to be remitted."


Plaintiff alleged that the higher tax rate thus placed on it, as a nonresident distributor without a commercial domicile within the State, is an undue and discriminatory burden upon interstate commerce.

Under G.S. 105-113.56A, resident distributors pay the tax due on a monthly report basis and are not required to affix crowns or stamps to the containers. As a nonresident, plaintiff is required to purchase and affix tax paid stamps or crowns to each soft drink container within twenty-four hours of its receipt into the State. Plaintiff alleged and offered evidence tending to show that this method was expensive, time consuming and otherwise burdensome.

The case [***3] was heard by the judge without a jury. In pertinent part, Judge Hobgood concluded and ordered as follows:

"That G.S. § 105-113.56A as applied to out-of-state distributors is violative of Article I, Section 8, Clause 3 (the Commerce Clause) of the United States Constitution in that it is an unreasonable burden on interstate commerce.

Now, Therefore, it is Hereby Ordered, Adjudged and Decreed that:

1. In respect to G.S. § 105-113.56A the word 'resident' and the clause 'and any distributor or wholesale dealer having a commercial domicile in this state' have caused the above statute to be violative of Article I, Section 8, Clause 3 of the Constitution of the United States; it is therefore declared that these words are null and void and are of no effect whatsoever.

2. The plaintiff be permitted to pay all taxes due on bottled soft drinks under the 'Soft Drink Tax Act' under the alternate method as provided in G.S. § 105-113.56A.

3. The plaintiff have and recover from the defendant the amount of $ 6,764.06, that being the amount of taxes, penalty and interest paid by the plaintiff 'under protest' to the defendant since June 9, 1972."


Defendant appealed.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, a nonresident distributor, brought an action against defendant North Carolina Commissioner of Revenue to recover certain taxes that it had paid under protest. Following a bench trial, the Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina) entered a judgment in favor of the distributor, finding that § 105-113.56A violated U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3 because it was an unreasonable burden on interstate commerce. The Commissioner appealed.


OVERVIEW: Section 105-113.56A required nonresident distributors to purchase and affix tax paid stamps or crowns to each soft drink container within 24 hours of its receipt into North Carolina. Yet, § 105-113.56A did not require resident distributors to affix crowns or stamps to the containers. Consequently, the nonresident distributor alleged that the higher tax rate placed on it was an undue and discriminatory burden upon interstate commerce. The trial court found that § 105-113.56A was unconstitutional as applied to the nonresident distributor. Thus, the nonresident distributor was awarded the amount of taxes, penalty, and interest that it paid "under protest." The Commissioner sought review. The court held that the application of § 105-113.56A to any "resident" distributor or wholesale dealer and to any distributor or wholesale dealer "having a commercial domicile in North Carolina" constituted an implicit provision that it did not apply to any "nonresident" distributor or wholesale dealer or to any distributor or wholesale dealer "not having a commercial domicile in North Carolina." The implied exclusion of nonresident distributors from § 105-113.56A was void under the Commerce Clause.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

State v. Clark


No. 741SC403


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


22 N.C. App. 81; 206 S.E.2d 252; 1974 N.C. App. LEXIS 2252


April 18, 1974, Argued in the Court of Appeals  
June 19, 1974, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendant from Copeland, Judge, 12 September 1973 Session of Superior Court held in Chowan County.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court, Chowan County (North Carolina), in which he was convicted of failing to comply with a lawful command to disperse.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of failing to comply with a lawful order. On appeal, defendant contended that the warrant should have been quashed because no probable cause for arrest was shown in the affidavit executed in support of the warrant for arrest. The affidavit was sufficient and clearly showed that it was made upon the personal knowledge of the sheriff. Defendant also argued tat he was unable to determine what section of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-288.4 he was charged with under the warrant. The court held that it was not necessary for the warrant to have been drawn under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-288.4. If defendant desired more definite information about the particular disorderly conduct the officer reasonably believed was occurring, he had the right to have requested a bill of particulars.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

1431 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


Sides v. Cabarrus Memorial Hospital, Inc.


No. 7419SC449


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


22 N.C. App. 117; 205 S.E.2d 784; 1974 N.C. App. LEXIS 2256


May 15, 1974, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
June 19, 1974, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendant, Cabarrus Memorial Hospital, Inc., from Exum, Judge, at the 3 September 1973 Session of Cabarrus Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant hospital appealed a judgment from the Cabarrus Superior Court (North Carolina) denying its motion for summary judgment against the action of plaintiff, administrator of the deceased's estate. The administrator filed a motion to dismiss this appeal on the basis that the hospital lacked a right of appeal.


OVERVIEW: The hospital contended that it was an agency of North Carolina, similar to a municipality or public corporation, that was distinct from the county where the hospital was located. Therefore, the hospital contended that the county's purchase of liability insurance was not binding on the hospital and did not waive the hospital's governmental immunity, which deprived the court of jurisdiction. The court affirmed the contested judgment. The court denied the administrator's motion because, although there was no general right of appeal, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1-277(b) provided the hospital with the right to appeal the ruling that the superior court had jurisdiction over the hospital. The court followed several previous rulings determining that the hospital was an agency of the county. The court ruled that § 9 of N.C. Pub.-Local & Priv. L. ch. 307 (1935) provided the hospital with legal corporate status. The hospital was a county agency, and it was bound by the county's purchase of insurance. The court rejected as meritless the hospital's contention that it could disavow the county's purchase of insurance and simultaneously accept the benefits of all the other county finances.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that denied the hospital's motion for summary judgment against the administrator's lawsuit. The administrator's motion to dismiss this appeal was denied.

 

1432 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


State v. Crouse


No. 7422SC335


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


22 N.C. App. 47; 205 S.E.2d 361; 1974 N.C. App. LEXIS 2232



June 5, 1974, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendant from Copeland, Judge, 26 November 1973 Session of Superior Court held in Davidson County.

Defendant Jessie Lee Crouse was indicted for committing a crime against nature.

The evidence for the State included the testimony of Teresa Jean Fulk, age seven, that on 1 April 1972, defendant committed sodomy on her; that after the act defendant bathed her, changed her clothes and then took her to her grandmother. The State also offered the testimony of several other witnesses with whom Teresa Jean had discussed the crime. A doctor testified that when he examined Teresa Jean several hours after the alleged attack, the anal opening was markedly red, and tests revealed the presence of sperm in the rectum.

Defendant denied his guilt, stating that Teresa Jean wanted to go shopping with him so he bathed her and changed her clothes and that he noticed some sores on her bottom and put lotion on them.

The jury found defendant guilty, and he was sentenced to a prison term of 10 years.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment, after a jury trial in the Criminal Session of Davidson County Superior Court (North Carolina), convicting him of a crime against nature, in violation of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-177. He asserted that the trial court erred in failing to quash the indictment because § 14-177 was unconstitutionally vague and the indictment had exposed him to double jeopardy. He also disputed the use of leading questions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of a crime against nature and appealed. He insisted that the trial court wrongly had refused to quash the indictment because § 14-177 was unconstitutional and had put him twice in jeopardy. He also contested the solicitor's use of leading questions to examine the complaining witness. The court disagreed, explaining that it had previously held that § 14-177 was constitutional. The court further noted that, formerly, defendant had been indicted for rape of the same victim. In that case the State took a nolle prosequi with leave at about the time that the indictment for crime against nature was returned. The court observed that rape and sodomy were distinct and separate crimes. Although both indictments against defendant involved the same transaction, they had not charged offenses which were the same in law and fact. Consequently, defendant had not been twice put in jeopardy for the same offense. Given the nature of the crime and the extreme youth of the complaining witness, the trial court had not abused its discretion in permitting the solicitor to ask her leading questions.


OUTCOME: The court found no error.

State v. Lisk


No. 743SC266


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


21 N.C. App. 474; 204 S.E.2d 868; 1974 N.C. App. LEXIS 1844


April 10, 1974, Argued in the Court of Appeals  
May 15, 1974, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Rouse, Judge, 1 October 1973 Criminal Session, Pitt County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment from the Criminal Session of the Pitt County Superior Court (North Carolina) that convicted him of an offense involving a controlled substance. The superior court declined to quash the State's bill of indictment on the basis that it was premised upon an unconstitutional statute, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 90-88. The superior court declined to require the State to disclose the identity of its confidential informant.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that § 90-88 provided constitutionally impermissible authority to the North Carolina Commission of Health Services. The court rejected as meritless defendant's contentions on the constitutionality of § 90-88. The court concluded that § 90-88 did not improperly delegate the legislature's authority to define crimes to the Commission by permitting it to add, delete, or reschedule a substance as a controlled substance. The court determined that the guidelines imposed by the legislature upon the rescheduling of controlled substances were more than adequate. The superior court correctly decided not to obligate the State to disclose the informant's identity. The court found that defendant failed to show either that the informant participated in the offense or that his identity would have been essential to the defense. Defendant's failure to include a search warrant in the record precluded the court from reviewing his contention that evidence was seized pursuant to an invalid warrant and thus was inadmissible. The court rejected as meritless defendant's contention that he was neither fully advised of his rights nor did he knowingly and intelligently waive them.


OUTCOME: The superior court committed no error.

Quick v. Charlotte


No. 7326SC698


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


21 N.C. App. 401; 204 S.E.2d 533; 1974 N.C. App. LEXIS 1815



May 1, 1974, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by plaintiffs from Snepp, Judge, 11 June 1973 Schedule "C" Civil Session of Superior Court held in Mecklenburg County.

The facts in this case are not in dispute. During 1968, the City of Charlotte embarked upon a five-year plan for the renovation and expansion of its Douglas Municipal Airport. Construction of additional runway facilities necessitated the acquisition, on 23 March 1971, of adjacent property owned by E. O. and Lillian Hudson and T. A. and Virginia Freeman. At the time of purchase, plaintiffs were month-to-month tenants of five houses located thereon. Plaintiffs continued to live on the Hudson-Freeman property until runway construction required the demolition of their rented houses in late November 1971.

There were, however, other rental units located on the Hudson-Freeman property which were not razed until 1972. The tenants of these dwellings were not forced off the Hudson-Freeman property until after 1 January 1972, and, by qualifying as "displaced persons" under G.S. 133-7(3), received assistance in the form of funds and services under the provisions of The Uniform Relocation Assistance and Real Property Acquisition Policies Act (Relocation Assistance [***2] Act), G.S. 133-5 et seq. Because the Relocation Assistance Act extends its benefits only to persons who, under the requisite circumstances, are forced to move themselves or their personalty from real property "on or after January 1, 1972," plaintiffs were denied such assistance.

On 25 September 1972, plaintiffs, alleging that the Relocation Assistance Act violated the equal protection clauses of the United States and North Carolina Constitutions, filed suit against defendant City of Charlotte to enjoin permanently "further application and disbursement of funds" under the Act and to recover actual and punitive damages resulting from their being denied the statutory benefits. The parties, stipulating to the above facts and waiving jury trial, submitted the case for trial solely upon the constitutional issue. Because the constitutionality of the North Carolina statute was at issue, the Attorney General of North Carolina successfully moved the court to be made a party to the action. The trial court, incorporating the stipulation of facts into its judgment, held that the Relocation Assistance Act was constitutional both on its face and as applied and dismissed plaintiffs' action. Plaintiffs [***3] appealed.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff tenants appealed a decision of the Mecklenburg County Superior Court (North Carolina) that dismissed their action alleging that the Relocation Assistance Act, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 133-5 et seq., was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The tenants were forced to re-locate as a result of an airport expansion project that required their homes to be demolished. However, they were not entitled to re-location assistance pursuant to the Act because they moved prior to the Act's effective date. The tenants brought this action alleging that the Act was discriminatory because it only assisted displaced persons who moved on or after a certain date. The trial court dismissed the action, finding that the Act was constitutional both on its face and as applied. The tenants appealed, and the court affirmed, holding that a statute that attempted to relieve a given form of public hardship must begin at some point, and the reasons given for the effective date at issue were credible.


OUTCOME: The decision of the trial court was affirmed.

In re Newsome
